The Hierarchy of Lights and the Oneness of God
Enumerates four luminous bodies and draws an analogical argument for divine oneness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the hierarchy of physical luminous bodies demonstrates the necessity of a single supreme light in the spiritual world, reinforcing the doctrine of tawhid.

Core Idea
Just as the sun outshines all bodies, the spiritual realm must have one supreme light.

The author begins the second type of proofs from spiritual unveiling by drawing a powerful analogy from the physical world. He classifies luminous bodies into four kinds and shows that the sun is the highest. Then he argues a fortiori that if the material world has a single greatest light, the purer spiritual world must have a single supreme light—pointing to the oneness of Allah.

Guided Path
1
Read the classification of luminous bodies
Identify the four luminous bodies: sun, moon, stars, and fire. Note how the author describes their ranks, with the sun as the highest.
The sun is described as the source of overpowering light; at its rising, other lights vanish.
2
Understand the analogy to the spiritual world
The author argues that since the spiritual world is purer and nobler, it must have a greatest light just as the physical world has the sun. This greatest light is unique.
This is an a fortiori (قَياس أَولى) argument.
3
Reflect on the properties of the greatest spiritual light
The passage states that the greatest spiritual light nullifies all other lights and existents by its grace and majesty. Consider how this relates to Allah's attributes.
This reinforces tawhid and tanzih.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النوع الثاني (١) من دلائل أصحاب المكاشفات، وأرباب المجاهدات:
قالوا: قد ثبت أن الموجودات إما روحانية، وإما جسمانية
أربعة:
·
فنقول: أما عالم الأجسام فهي إما نيرة وإما مظلمة. وأما النيرات فهي الشمس، والقمر، والكواكب، والنار. وأعلى هذه الأربعة وأشرفها هو الشمس، لأنها ينبوع النور القاهر، والضوء الظاهر وعند طلوعها تضمحل يراه. الذي له الكرامة والقدرة الأبدية [١ تيمو ٩ : ١٥ - ١٩].
ثالثاً: لقد ظهر مما تقدم أن عقيدة التوحيد مع التنزيه. صرحت بها التوراة، وصرح بها الإنجيل، وصرح بها القرآن الكريم. وإذا كان الأمر كذلك. فلماذا يقول النصارى بالتجسد وبالتعدد. وليس على التجسد والتعدد دليل من التوراة، أو دليل من الإنجيل؟ إن التوراة - رغم تحريفها - تشهد بالتوحيد وبالتنزيه. والأناجيل - رغم تحريفها - تشهد بالتوحيد وبالتنزيه. فمن أين إذن عقيدة التجسد وعقيدة التعدد؟
والإجابة على ذلك: تدل كتب التواريخ على أن الإمبراطور الروماني، قسطنطين، جمع النصارى في مدينة (نيقية: وهي: تركيا، الآن. في سنة ثلثمائة وخمسة وعشرين من ميلاد المسيح، وأراد منهم أن يقولوا بدين فيه عقائد الرومان وفيه عقائد النصارى. أي يوحدوا الديانتين في ديانة
واحدة. وكان النصاري يضطهدون من قبل ملوك الرومان لئلا يظهروا أن نبيا بشر به المسيح سيأتي وسيمتد ملكه إلى أقصى الأرض. وسوف يزيل نفوذ الرومان من العالم. ولقد رضي النصاري القليلو الإيمان بذلك. واتفقوا على «قانون الإيمان»، وفيه: الاعتراف بأن الله واحد، وأن المسيح إله ثان ثم في مدينة القسطنطينية، سنة ثلثمائة وواحد وثمانين اتفقوا على أن (الروح القدس)، إله ثالث وفي مجمع خليقدونية، على شاطيء البسفور، في سنة أربعمائة وواحد وحين انقسم النصارى إلى أرثوذكس وكاتوليك. وظل الانقسام إلى اليوم. فالكاثوليك قالوا بالألهة الثلاثة. والأرثوذكس قالوا بإله واحد قد تجسم في صورة المسيح، وهذا الكلام ثابت من كتب تواريخ النصارى أنفسهم، ولا يشك أحد منهم في صدقه
ولو أن النصارى اليوم، وقد خف عنهم عذاب الرومان. رجعوا إلى كتبهم المقدسة التي بين ايديهم رغم تحريفها. لوجدوا فيها كما قلنا: عقيدة التوحيد والتنزيه. وأن المسيح بن مريم رسول من رسل الله، ونبي من أنبياء الله مثل هارون وإلياس واليسع - عليهم السلام - وعندئذ يتفقون مع اليهود ومع المسلمين في عقيدة التوحيد والتنزيه. وإذا أصروا على عقيدة التجسد، وعقيدة التعدد فإنهم لا يتفقوا أبدا مع المسلمين، ولا مع المسيح نفسه، ولا مع موسى في بني إسرائيل في التوحيد والتنزيه. والله أعلم (1) لاحظ أنهم أربعة أنواع.
٢٦٩

الأضواء، وتبطل الأنوار، وأما القمر فهو كالوزير له، ولهذا السبب صار سلطان الليل، وأما الكواكب فإنها مرتبة على سبع درجات، فأقواها في القوة وأعظمها في الجثة ما يكون في حد العظم الأول، ولا يزال يتناقص حتى ينتهي إلى العظم [السابع (۱)] فيصير في غاية الصغر، وأما النار فإنها نير العالم السفلي ونورها ضعيف جدا من وجوه:
أحدهما: أنها وإن قويت وعظمت، إلا أنها تنطفىء بأدنى سبب
وثانيها: أنها وإن كانت تفيد الإشراف إلا أنها تفيد الإحراق .
وثالثها: أن نورها ممتزج بالدخان، وأن استعلاءها قليل، ويعلوها الدخان والظلمة، والكدورة . قالوا: فهذه مراتب النيرات في عالم الأجسام . وإذا عرفت هذا فنقول : قد ظهر أن عالم الأرواح أصفى وأكمل وأشرف من عالم الأجسام ، فلما حصلت هذه النيرات في عالم الأجسام ، فبأن تحصل النيرات في عالم الأرواح، كان أولى . وكما أن درجات النيرات في عالم الأجسام متفاوتة في الكمال والنقصان ، وكان أعظم شيء: واحد هو الشمس . فكذلك يجب أن يكون النير الأعظم في عالم الأرواح واحد فقط . وكما أن من صفة الخير الأعظم من عالم الأجسام أن عند طلوعه تبطل أنوار سائر النيرات وتبطل الظلمة عن الظلمانيات ، فيصير النير مظلما والمظلم نيرا . فكذلك النير الأعظم في عالم الأرواح (۲) [واحد فقط ، وكما أن من صفة النير الأعظم في عالم الأجسام أن عند طلوعه تبطل أنوار سائر النيرات، وتبطل الظلمة عن الظلمانيات ، فيصير النير مظلما ، والمظلم نيرا ، فكذلك الأعظم في عالم الروحانيات ] (٣) يبطل في نوره کل نور ، ويبطل في إشراقة وجوده كل موجود. فبكمال فضله ورحمته ، يصير كل معدوم كالموجود ، وبقوة قهر جلاله يصير كل موجود كالمعدوم . فهذه أحوال
The second type (1) of proofs of the people of unveilings and the masters of spiritual exertions: They said: It is established that existents are either spiritual or corporeal. Four. And we say: As for the world of bodies, they are either luminous or dark. As for the luminous ones, they are the sun, the moon, the stars, and fire. The highest and most noble of these four is the sun, because it is the source of overpowering light and apparent brightness, and at its rising disappears... who has glory and eternal power [1 Timothy 9:15-19]. Thirdly: It has become clear from what preceded that the creed of tawhid (monotheism) together with tanzih (transcendence) was stated clearly by the Torah, stated clearly by the Gospel, and stated clearly by the Noble Quran. If that is so, why do the Christians speak of incarnation (tajassud) and trinity (ta‘addud)? There is no evidence for incarnation and trinity from the Torah or from the Gospel. Indeed, the Torah—despite its distortion—bears witness to tawhid and tanzih. And the Gospels—despite their distortion—bear witness to tawhid and tanzih. So from where then the creed of incarnation and the creed of trinity? The answer to that: The books of history indicate that the Roman emperor Constantine gathered the Christians in the city of Nicaea (which is in Turkey now) in the year 325 of the Christian era, and wanted them to profess a religion containing the beliefs of the Romans and the beliefs of the Christians, i.e., to unify the two religions into one religion. And the Christians were being persecuted by the Roman kings lest they show that a prophet announced by Christ would come and his kingdom would extend to the ends of the earth, and would remove the Roman influence from the world. And the Christians of weak faith agreed to that. And they agreed on the 'Creed,' which includes the acknowledgment that God is one, and that Christ is a second god. Then in the city of Constantinople, in the year 381, they agreed that the Holy Spirit is a third god. And in the Council of Chalcedon, on the shore of the Bosphorus, in the year 451, the Christians split into Orthodox and Catholic. The division remains to this day. The Catholics said there are three gods. The Orthodox said there is one God who incarnated in the form of Christ. This statement is established from the historical books of the Christians themselves, and none of them doubts its truth. If only the Christians today, now that the persecution of the Romans has been lifted from them, would return to their sacred books that are in their hands despite their distortion, they would find in them, as we said, the creed of tawhid and tanzih, and that Christ son of Mary is a messenger from the messengers of God and a prophet from the prophets of God, like Aaron, Elijah, and Elisha—peace be upon them. Then they would agree with the Jews and with the Muslims on the creed of tawhid and tanzih. And if they insist on the creed of incarnation and the creed of trinity, they will never agree with the Muslims, nor with Christ himself, nor with Moses among the Children of Israel, on tawhid and tanzih. And God knows best. (1) Note that they are four types. 269. The lights, and the lights become null. As for the moon, it is like a minister to it, and for this reason it became the sultan of the night. As for the stars, they are arranged in seven degrees: the strongest in power and greatest in body is what is in the first degree, and it continues decreasing until it reaches the seventh degree, becoming extremely small. As for fire, it is the light of the lower world, and its light is very weak in several respects: One: even if it becomes strong and great, it goes out with the slightest cause. Second: even if it provides illumination, it provides burning. Third: its light is mixed with smoke, its ascension is little, and smoke, darkness, and turbidity rise above it. They said: These are the ranks of luminous bodies in the world of bodies. And if you know this, we say: It has become clear that the world of spirits is purer, more perfect, and nobler than the world of bodies. Since these luminous bodies occur in the world of bodies, then that luminous bodies occur in the world of spirits is more fitting. And just as the degrees of luminous bodies in the world of bodies are varying in perfection and deficiency, and the greatest thing is one, namely the sun, so likewise the greatest light in the world of spirits must be only one. And just as it is a characteristic of the greatest good in the world of bodies that at its rising the lights of all other luminous bodies become null, and darkness becomes null for dark things, so that the luminous becomes dark and the dark becomes luminous, so likewise the greatest light in the world of spirits is only one. And just as it is a characteristic of the greatest light in the world of bodies that at its rising the lights of all other luminous bodies become null, and darkness becomes null for dark things, so that the luminous becomes dark and the dark becomes luminous, so likewise the greatest in the world of spiritual beings nullifies in its light every light, and nullifies in the radiance of its existence every existent. And by the perfection of His grace and mercy, every non-existent becomes like existent, and by the power of the overwhelmingness of His majesty, every existent becomes like non-existent. These are the states.
Source pages p. 270

Commentary
This passage is a combined presentation of two distinct proofs under the second type of arguments from those who possess spiritual unveiling (ahl al-mukāshafāt) and spiritual exertions (arbāb al-mujāhadāt). The author first presents a cosmological analogy based on the hierarchy of luminous bodies in the physical world, then transitions to a historical refutation of the Christian Trinity.

The first part begins by classifying all existents into spiritual (rūḥāniyya) and corporeal (jismāniyya). The corporeal world is further divided into luminous (nīra) and dark (muẓlima). The luminous bodies are four: the sun, the moon, the stars, and fire. The sun is the highest and most noble because it is the source of overpowering light and apparent brightness; at its rising, other lights vanish. The author then describes the moon as a minister to the sun and ruler of the night, the stars as arranged in seven degrees of decreasing size and power, and fire as the light of the lower world with three weaknesses: it is easily extinguished, it burns while illuminating, and its light is mixed with smoke and darkness.

After this classification, the author argues a fortiori: if the spiritual world is purer, more perfect, and nobler than the corporeal world, then the existence of luminous bodies in the spiritual world is even more fitting. Just as the degrees of luminous bodies in the corporeal world are varying and the greatest is one (the sun), so the greatest light (al-nayr al-aʿẓam) in the spiritual world must be only one. The author then draws an analogy: just as the rising of the sun nullifies all other lights and transforms darkness into light, so the greatest spiritual light nullifies all other lights and existents through its perfection, mercy, and overwhelming majesty.

The second part of the passage constitutes a third point (thālithan) that builds on what preceded. The author asserts that the creed of tawḥīd (monotheism) and tanzīh (divine transcendence) is clearly stated in the Torah, the Gospel, and the Quran. He then asks why Christians profess incarnation (tajassud) and trinity (taʿaddud) when there is no evidence for these in their scriptures. The answer is historical: Emperor Constantine gathered the Christians at Nicaea in 325 CE to unify Roman and Christian beliefs. Protestants? The Christians were being persecuted because they were expected to foretell a prophet who would remove Roman power. Weak-faithed Christians agreed, and the Council of Nicaea produced a creed acknowledging God as one but Christ as a second god. Later, in Constantinople in 381, the Holy Spirit was added as a third god. At Chalcedon in 451, the church split into Catholic (three gods) and Orthodox (one God incarnated). The author notes that these historical facts are attested by Christian historians themselves. He concludes by urging Christians today to return to their scriptures, where they would find tawḥīd and tanzīh, and to recognize Jesus as a messenger and prophet like others. If they insist on incarnation and trinity, they will not agree with Muslims, Christ, or Moses.

In summary, the passage combines a rational cosmological proof for the oneness of God based on the hierarchy of lights with a historical argument that the Christian Trinity is a later human invention, not original revelation.

Key Terms
النيرات
عالم الأجسام
عالم الأرواح
النير الأعظم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the argument is merely poetic analogy, not a rational proof.
Correction: The author presents it as a necessary rational inference (qiyas awla): if the imperfect physical world has a single supreme light, then the perfect spiritual world must have a single supreme light.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the highest of the four luminous bodies in the physical world?
A) The moon
B) The sun
C) The stars
D) Fire

Key Takeaway
The physical hierarchy of lights points to a single supreme light in the spiritual realm, affirming tawhid.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
لقد قرأت أن النير الأعظم في عالم الأرواح يبطل في نوره كل نور. هل تعيش حقاً كما لو أن وجود الله يلغي أهمية كل ما سواه في حياتك؟ في أي مجال من حياتك ما زلت تتعامل مع شيء وكأنه مستقل عن قدرته؟ ما هي الخطوة التي ستتخذها اليوم لترسيخ هذه الحقيقة في قلبك؟
?
يذكر النص أن النصارى تنازلوا عن التوحيد من أجل السلامة من الاضطهاد الروماني. هل هناك مواقف في حياتك تتنازل فيها عن حقيقة التوحيد في أقوالك أو أفعالك خوفاً من ردة فعل الناس؟ ما هو الموقف المحدد الذي ستواجهه غداً بإخلاص التوحيد مهما كلفك؟
?
إذا كنت تؤمن بأن النير الأعظم هو الله وحده، فهل رتبت أولويات حياتك وفق هذا الإيمان؟ اذكر شيئاً واحداً في يومك يستهلك وقتك وجهدك أكثر من الله، وقرر كيف ستجعله في مرتبته الصحيحة بدءاً من اليوم.

Allah's Existence
intermediate
5 min
Historical Origins of the Christian Trinity
Historical refutation of Trinity as later church invention.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the historical development of the Trinity doctrine through church councils and why the author argues it is a later invention, not original revelation.

Core Idea
The Trinity was invented at Nicaea and later councils, not found in original scriptures.

After establishing the rational proof for tawhid, the author turns to a historical argument. He notes that the Torah, Gospel, and Quran all teach monotheism and transcendence. He then asks why Christians profess incarnation and trinity. The answer lies in church history: Emperor Constantine engineered the doctrine at Nicaea, and subsequent councils solidified it. The author urges Christians to return to their scriptures, which still bear witness to tawhid.

Guided Path
1
Read the author's claim about scriptures
Notice that the author asserts Torah, Gospel, and Quran all teach tawhid and tanzih, even despite distortions.
This sets up the question: why do Christians differ?
2
Follow the historical narrative
Trace the events: Council of Nicaea (325 CE), Constantinople (381 CE), Chalcedon (451 CE). Understand how each council added to the Trinity doctrine.
Constantine wanted to unify Roman and Christian beliefs.
3
Understand the author's conclusion
The author urges Christians to return to original scripture, accept Jesus as a prophet, and thereby agree with Muslims and Jews on tawhid.
This is a da'wah-oriented conclusion.

النوع الثاني (١) من دلائل أصحاب المكاشفات، وأرباب المجاهدات:
قالوا: قد ثبت أن الموجودات إما روحانية، وإما جسمانية
أربعة:
·
فنقول: أما عالم الأجسام فهي إما نيرة وإما مظلمة. وأما النيرات فهي الشمس، والقمر، والكواكب، والنار. وأعلى هذه الأربعة وأشرفها هو الشمس، لأنها ينبوع النور القاهر، والضوء الظاهر وعند طلوعها تضمحل يراه. الذي له الكرامة والقدرة الأبدية [١ تيمو ٩ : ١٥ - ١٩].

The second type (1) of proofs of the people of unveilings and the masters of spiritual exertions: They said: It is established that existents are either spiritual or corporeal. Four. And we say: As for the world of bodies, they are either luminous or dark. As for the luminous ones, they are the sun, the moon, the stars, and fire. The highest and most noble of these four is the sun, because it is the source of overpowering light and apparent brightness, and at its rising disappears... who has glory and eternal power [1 Timothy 9:15-19]. Thirdly: It has become clear from what preceded that the creed of tawhid (monotheism) together with tanzih (transcendence) was stated clearly by the Torah, stated clearly by the Gospel, and stated clearly by the Noble Quran. If that is so, why do the Christians speak of incarnation (tajassud) and trinity (ta‘addud)? There is no evidence for incarnation and trinity from the Torah or from the Gospel. Indeed, the Torah—despite its distortion—bears witness to tawhid and tanzih. And the Gospels—despite their distortion—bear witness to tawhid and tanzih. So from where then the creed of incarnation and the creed of trinity? The answer to that: The books of history indicate that the Roman emperor Constantine gathered the Christians in the city of Nicaea (which is in Turkey now) in the year 325 of the Christian era, and wanted them to profess a religion containing the beliefs of the Romans and the beliefs of the Christians, i.e., to unify the two religions into one religion. And the Christians were being persecuted by the Roman kings lest they show that a prophet announced by Christ would come and his kingdom would extend to the ends of the earth, and would remove the Roman influence from the world. And the Christians of weak faith agreed to that. And they agreed on the 'Creed,' which includes the acknowledgment that God is one, and that Christ is a second god. Then in the city of Constantinople, in the year 381, they agreed that the Holy Spirit is a third god. And in the Council of Chalcedon, on the shore of the Bosphorus, in the year 451, the Christians split into Orthodox and Catholic. The division remains to this day. The Catholics said there are three gods. The Orthodox said there is one God who incarnated in the form of Christ. This statement is established from the historical books of the Christians themselves, and none of them doubts its truth. If only the Christians today, now that the persecution of the Romans has been lifted from them, would return to their sacred books that are in their hands despite their distortion, they would find in them, as we said, the creed of tawhid and tanzih, and that Christ son of Mary is a messenger from the messengers of God and a prophet from the prophets of God, like Aaron, Elijah, and Elisha—peace be upon them. Then they would agree with the Jews and with the Muslims on the creed of tawhid and tanzih. And if they insist on the creed of incarnation and the creed of trinity, they will never agree with the Muslims, nor with Christ himself, nor with Moses among the Children of Israel, on tawhid and tanzih. And God knows best. (1) Note that they are four types. 269. The lights, and the lights become null. As for the moon, it is like a minister to it, and for this reason it became the sultan of the night. As for the stars, they are arranged in seven degrees: the strongest in power and greatest in body is what is in the first degree, and it continues decreasing until it reaches the seventh degree, becoming extremely small. As for fire, it is the light of the lower world, and its light is very weak in several respects: One: even if it becomes strong and great, it goes out with the slightest cause. Second: even if it provides illumination, it provides burning. Third: its light is mixed with smoke, its ascension is little, and smoke, darkness, and turbidity rise above it. They said: These are the ranks of luminous bodies in the world of bodies. And if you know this, we say: It has become clear that the world of spirits is purer, more perfect, and nobler than the world of bodies. Since these luminous bodies occur in the world of bodies, then that luminous bodies occur in the world of spirits is more fitting. And just as the degrees of luminous bodies in the world of bodies are varying in perfection and deficiency, and the greatest thing is one, namely the sun, so likewise the greatest light in the world of spirits must be only one. And just as it is a characteristic of the greatest good in the world of bodies that at its rising the lights of all other luminous bodies become null, and darkness becomes null for dark things, so that the luminous becomes dark and the dark becomes luminous, so likewise the greatest light in the world of spirits is only one. And just as it is a characteristic of the greatest light in the world of bodies that at its rising the lights of all other luminous bodies become null, and darkness becomes null for dark things, so that the luminous becomes dark and the dark becomes luminous, so likewise the greatest in the world of spiritual beings nullifies in its light every light, and nullifies in the radiance of its existence every existent. And by the perfection of His grace and mercy, every non-existent becomes like existent, and by the power of the overwhelmingness of His majesty, every existent becomes like non-existent. These are the states.
Source pages p. 270

Commentary
This passage is a combined presentation of two distinct proofs under the second type of arguments from those who possess spiritual unveiling (ahl al-mukāshafāt) and spiritual exertions (arbāb al-mujāhadāt). The author first presents a cosmological analogy based on the hierarchy of luminous bodies in the physical world, then transitions to a historical refutation of the Christian Trinity.

The first part begins by classifying all existents into spiritual (rūḥāniyya) and corporeal (jismāniyya). The corporeal world is further divided into luminous (nīra) and dark (muẓlima). The luminous bodies are four: the sun, the moon, the stars, and fire. The sun is the highest and most noble because it is the source of overpowering light and apparent brightness; at its rising, other lights vanish. The author then describes the moon as a minister to the sun and ruler of the night, the stars as arranged in seven degrees of decreasing size and power, and fire as the light of the lower world with three weaknesses: it is easily extinguished, it burns while illuminating, and its light is mixed with smoke and darkness.

After this classification, the author argues a fortiori: if the spiritual world is purer, more perfect, and nobler than the corporeal world, then the existence of luminous bodies in the spiritual world is even more fitting. Just as the degrees of luminous bodies in the corporeal world are varying and the greatest is one (the sun), so the greatest light (al-nayr al-aʿẓam) in the spiritual world must be only one. The author then draws an analogy: just as the rising of the sun nullifies all other lights and transforms darkness into light, so the greatest spiritual light nullifies all other lights and existents through its perfection, mercy, and overwhelming majesty.

The second part of the passage constitutes a third point (thālithan) that builds on what preceded. The author asserts that the creed of tawḥīd (monotheism) and tanzīh (divine transcendence) is clearly stated in the Torah, the Gospel, and the Quran. He then asks why Christians profess incarnation (tajassud) and trinity (taʿaddud) when there is no evidence for these in their scriptures. The answer is historical: Emperor Constantine gathered the Christians at Nicaea in 325 CE to unify Roman and Christian beliefs. Protestants? The Christians were being persecuted because they were expected to foretell a prophet who would remove Roman power. Weak-faithed Christians agreed, and the Council of Nicaea produced a creed acknowledging God as one but Christ as a second god. Later, in Constantinople in 381, the Holy Spirit was added as a third god. At Chalcedon in 451, the church split into Catholic (three gods) and Orthodox (one God incarnated). The author notes that these historical facts are attested by Christian historians themselves. He concludes by urging Christians today to return to their scriptures, where they would find tawḥīd and tanzīh, and to recognize Jesus as a messenger and prophet like others. If they insist on incarnation and trinity, they will not agree with Muslims, Christ, or Moses.

In summary, the passage combines a rational cosmological proof for the oneness of God based on the hierarchy of lights with a historical argument that the Christian Trinity is a later human invention, not original revelation.

Correction: The passage argues that Trinity was formulated in later church councils (4th century) and is absent from original scriptures.

?
The passage talks about Christians of 'weak faith' who compromised under persecution. Is there an area in your life where you are compromising your faith to avoid discomfort, rejection, or losing status? How can you strengthen your resolve?
?
If you had to strip away everything added to your faith by later councils, synods, or scholars, and return to only what the original scriptures explicitly teach, would your belief system change? What would you lose? What would you gain?

Analogy of Physical Lights to the Supreme Spiritual Light
Analogical argument from physical lights to a single supreme spiritual light.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the hierarchy of physical lights leads to the rational conclusion that there is a single supreme spiritual light, namely God.

Core Idea
The sun's rule over physical lights points to one Supreme Light in the spiritual world.

This lesson presents a powerful analogical argument for the existence of God. By observing the ordered hierarchy of lights in the physical world—from the sun to the faintest star and fire—the author argues that the spiritual world, being more perfect, must have a single supreme light. This argument uses the principle of 'first analogy' (قياس أولى) and is meant to be evident to sound minds.

Guided Path
1
Identify the hierarchy of physical lights
Read the description of the sun, moon, planets, and fire. Notice how each has a specific rank and property.
The author emphasizes that the sun is the greatest and its rising nullifies other lights.
2
Understand the analogy to the spiritual world
The author then argues: if such a hierarchy exists in the lower physical world, the higher spiritual world must have an even greater single light.
This is based on the principle that the spiritual world is purer and more perfect.
3
Recognize the conclusion about God
The single greatest spiritual light is identified as the source of all existence, nullifying all other lights by its radiance.
The passage also mentions that spiritual exercises can lead to direct insight into this truth.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأضواء، وتبطل الأنوار، وأما القمر فهو كالوزير له، ولهذا السبب صار سلطان الليل، وأما الكواكب فإنها مرتبة على سبع درجات، فأقواها في القوة وأعظمها في الجثة ما يكون في حد العظم الأول، ولا يزال يتناقص حتى ينتهي إلى العظم السابع فيصير في غاية الصغر، وأما النار فإنها نير العالم السفلي ونورها ضعيف جدا من وجوه: أحدهما: أنها وإن قويت وعظمت، إلا أنها تنطفىء بأدنى سبب. وثانيها: أنها وإن كانت تفيد الإشراف إلا أنها تفيد الإحراق. وثالثها: أن نورها ممتزج بالدخان، وأن استعلاءها قليل، ويعلوها الدخان والظلمة، والكدورة. قالوا: فهذه مراتب النيرات في عالم الأجسام. وإذا عرفت هذا فنقول: قد ظهر أن عالم الأرواح أصفى وأكمل وأشرف من عالم الأجسام، فلما حصلت هذه النيرات في عالم الأجسام، فبأن تحصل النيرات في عالم الأرواح، كان أولى. وكما أن درجات النيرات في عالم الأجسام متفاوتة في الكمال والنقصان، وكان أعظم شيء: واحد هو الشمس. فكذلك يجب أن يكون النير الأعظم في عالم الأرواح واحد فقط. وكما أن من صفة الخير الأعظم من عالم الأجسام أن عند طلوعه تبطل أنوار سائر النيرات وتبطل الظلمة عن الظلمانيات، فيصير النير مظلما والمظلم نيرا. فكذلك النير الأعظم في عالم الأرواح واحد فقط، وكما أن من صفة النير الأعظم في عالم الأجسام أن عند طلوعه تبطل أنوار سائر النيرات، وتبطل الظلمة عن الظلمانيات، فيصير النير مظلما، والمظلم نيرا، فكذلك الأعظم في عالم الروحانيات يبطل في نوره كل نور، ويبطل في إشراقة وجوده كل موجود. فبكمال فضله ورحمته، يصير كل معدوم كالموجود، وبقوة قهر جلاله يصير كل موجود كالمعدوم. فهذه أحوال قريبة عند الأذهان السليمة، والعقول الصافية. ومن خاص في مقام الرياضات، وكانت نفسه في أصل الفطرة مشرقة علوية إلهية أبصر بعين بصيرته كل ما ذكرناه، إبصارا لا شك له فيه. والله ولي الإرشاد.
The lights, and the lights are extinguished. As for the moon, it is like a minister to it, and for this reason it became the sultan of the night. As for the planets, they are arranged in seven degrees. The strongest in power and greatest in body is what is at the limit of the first magnitude, and it continues decreasing until it reaches the seventh magnitude, becoming extremely small. As for fire, it is the light of the lower world and its light is very weak in several respects: First, even if it is strong and great, it is extinguished by the slightest cause. Second, even if it confers illumination, it confers burning. Third, its light is mixed with smoke, its ascent is slight, and smoke, darkness, and turbidity rise above it. They said: These are the ranks of lights in the world of bodies. And if you know this, we say: It has become apparent that the world of spirits is purer, more perfect, and nobler than the world of bodies. Since these lights exist in the world of bodies, then it is more fitting that lights exist in the world of spirits. And just as the degrees of lights in the world of bodies vary in perfection and deficiency, and the greatest thing is one, which is the sun, so too must the greatest light in the world of spirits be only one. And just as it is a characteristic of the greatest good in the world of bodies that upon its rising the lights of all other lights are nullified and darkness is removed from dark things, so that the luminous becomes dark and the dark becomes luminous, so too the greatest light in the world of spirits is only one. And just as it is a characteristic of the greatest light in the world of bodies that upon its rising the lights of all other lights are nullified and darkness is removed from dark things, so that the luminous becomes dark and the dark becomes luminous, so too the greatest in the world of spiritual beings nullifies in its light every light, and nullifies in the radiance of its existence every existent. By the perfection of His bounty and mercy, every non-existent becomes like existent, and by the power of the overwhelmingness of His majesty, every existent becomes like non-existent. These are states that are near to sound minds and pure intellects. And whoever is special in the station of spiritual exercises, and his soul was in the original disposition luminous, high, divine, sees with the eye of his insight everything we have mentioned, a seeing in which there is no doubt. And God is the Guide.

Commentary
This passage presents an analogical proof for the existence of a single supreme spiritual being (God) based on the hierarchy of lights in the physical world. The author first describes the ranks of physical lights: the sun is the greatest, the moon is like its minister, the planets are arranged in seven degrees of size and power decreasing to very small, and fire is the light of the lower world but is weak because it is easily extinguished, causes burning, and is mixed with smoke and darkness. These are the 'ranks of lights in the world of bodies.' Then the author argues that since the world of spirits is purer and more perfect than the world of bodies, it is even more fitting that there are lights in the spiritual world. Furthermore, just as the physical lights have varying degrees with a single greatest light (the sun), the spiritual world must have a single greatest light. Moreover, just as the sun's rising nullifies the lights of other celestial bodies and removes darkness, so this greatest spiritual light nullifies all other lights and makes all existents appear as non-existent in comparison. The passage concludes that these truths are evident to sound minds, and those who have purified themselves through spiritual exercises can directly witness this reality.

Proof from Innate Supplication in Times of Distress
Proof from the natural turning of skeptics to God when faced with calamities.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the universal involuntary turn to God in moments of calamity serves as a proof for the existence of a Wise Creator, even among those who outwardly deny Him.

Core Idea
Involuntary supplication in distress reveals an instinctive acknowledgment of God.

This lesson presents a powerful argument rooted in human nature: no matter how deeply someone entrenches themselves in atheism or skepticism, when faced with a terrifying calamity, their heart instinctively turns to God in supplication. This involuntary reaction, observable across all times and cultures, points to an innate awareness of a Supreme Being. The author supports this with a Quranic verse and concludes that sound intellects cannot escape this recognition.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow. Then read the translation. Pay attention to the author's description of the skeptics' behavior before, during, and after the calamity.
Notice the shift from intellectual denial to spontaneous supplication.
2
Identify the key observation
Find the core observation: that even those who work to deny God find themselves supplicating and showing humility when a disaster strikes. This is the basis of the proof.
3
Understand the logical reasoning
See how the author uses induction (استقراء) to claim this state is known necessarily. Ask yourself: why does this universal reaction imply a real object of supplication?
The argument relies on the spontaneity and universality of the reaction.
4
Reflect on the Quranic support
Read the cited verse from Surah al-'Ankabut (29:65). Notice how it mirrors the argument: people call on God sincerely when in danger, but forget Him when safe. This reinforces the idea of innate recognition.
5
Grasp the conclusion
The author concludes that all sound intellects recognize the existence of the Wise God, even if they later suppress it. Consider how this refutes the skeptic's claim that disbelief is purely rational.
The proof does not depend on sincerity; it works even for the most hardened denier.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النوع الثالث من دلائل أصحاب الرياضات: أن قالوا: إن أصحاب الشكوك والشبهات، وإن اجتهدوا في تقرير الخيالات الباطلة، وتأكيد الشبهات الفاسدة في نفي الإله المدبر، إلا أنه إذا نزلت بهم حادثة مؤلمة وواقعة مهيبة، فإنهم يجدون من صريح عقولهم وقلوبهم: التضرع، وإظهار الخضوع لإله العالم، والطلب منه أن يخلصهم من تلك البلية، ويخرجهم من تلك المحنة. ووجدان هذه الحالة كالأمر المعلوم بالضرورة بالاستقراء والاعتبار. ثم بعد الخلاص من تلك البلية، ربما عادوا إلى تقرير الشبهات، وإيراد الخيالات. وإليه الإشارة في القرآن بقوله: (فإذا ركبوا في الفلك دعوا الله مخلصين له الدين فلما نجاهم إلى البر إذا هم يشركون). وهذا يدل على أن جميع العقول السليمة، مقرة بوجود الإله الحكيم.
The third type of proofs from the people of spiritual exercises: They said: Indeed, the people of doubts and misconceptions, even if they strive to establish false fantasies and confirm corrupt misconceptions in denying the directing deity, yet when a painful event or a terrifying occurrence befalls them, they find in their explicit intellects and hearts: supplication, showing humility to the Lord of the world, and asking Him to save them from that calamity and deliver them from that ordeal. And the finding of this state is like a matter known necessarily by induction and reflection. Then after deliverance from that calamity, they perhaps return to establishing misconceptions and presenting fantasies. And to this refers the Quran in His saying: (So when they board the ship, they call upon Allah, making their religion sincere to Him. But when He delivers them to the land, at once they associate others with Him). And this indicates that all sound intellects acknowledge the existence of the Wise God.

Commentary
This passage presents the third type of proof from the spiritual exercises tradition. It observes that even those who are deeply skeptical and actively deny God's existence, when faced with a severe and terrifying event, spontaneously find themselves supplicating and showing humility before the Lord of the universe, asking for deliverance. This reaction is so universal that it is known necessarily by induction: no matter how much they intellectualize denial, in moments of genuine crisis their hearts turn to God. The author supports this with the Quranic verse from Surah al-'Ankabut (29:65) which describes people calling upon God sincerely when in danger at sea, only to revert to associating partners with Him once safe. The conclusion is that this innate response proves that all sound minds are in fact acknowledging the existence of a wise God, even if they later suppress it.

After He relieved you, did you gradually slip back into negligence or disobedience? ?

In what specific areas—your work, your relationships, your private moments—do you act as if you have forgotten that God is always with you? Proof from Answered Prayers
Proof from empirical observation of answered prayers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the observed phenomenon of answered prayers—especially for the distressed—provides an empirical proof for the existence of a responsive God.

Core Idea
Answered prayers of the distressed are an observable sign pointing to a God who hears and responds.

This passage presents a unique argument for God's existence rooted in spiritual experience: the universal observation that prayers of the distressed are answered. It is not a theoretical proof but an empirical one, accessible to anyone through repeated testing. The author connects the strength of this phenomenon to the purity of the soul, showing a link between spiritual refinement and the experience of divine response. Let us unpack this argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the English translation. Notice how the argument builds from observation to conclusion.
The passage is incomplete, but the main argument is clear.
2
Identify the empirical claim
Find the phrase stating what 'everyone knows by the validity of experience and testing.' This is the core evidence: prayers of the distressed are answered.
3
Connect to the theological conclusion
Notice how the author moves from observed phenomenon to the existence of a God who hears and answers. Ask yourself: why does this empirical fact imply a divine responder?
The commentary will explain the logical steps.

The fourth type of proofs from the people of spiritual exercises and unveilings: That which everyone knows by the validity of experience and testing from the answering of the prayer of the distressed, the relief of the aggrieved, the helping of the oppressed against the oppressors. And that indicates that this world has a God who hears the supplication and answers the call. He, exalted, is the ultimate object of the seeking of needs, and from Him is the attainment of requests. So the hands are raised to Him, the eyes are humbled before Him, the necks submit to His power, and the tongues are honored by the mention of His majesty. He is sought for sufficiency, and not sought beyond Him; desire is directed to Him, and not away from Him; and the needs of the needy never cease from His presence, and not...
Source pages p. 272

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth type of proof from the spiritual exercises and unveilings tradition. It argues from empirical observation: everyone knows through repeated experience that the prayers of those in distress are answered, the aggrieved are relieved, and the oppressed are aided against oppressors. The author notes that the effectiveness of this is proportional to the purity of the soul and strength of the spirit, indicating that those more connected to the spiritual realm experience this more strongly. This empirical data points to the existence of a God who hears and answers. The passage then elaborates on the implications: all hands are raised to Him, eyes are humbled, necks submit, and tongues are honored by mentioning His majesty. He is the ultimate source of sufficiency and the sole object of desire. The text cuts off but begins a statement that needs never cease from His presence.

Key Terms
المكاشفات
إجابة الدعاء
المضطرين
الملهوفين
الروحانيات
الجسمانيات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This proof claims that every specific prayer is answered exactly as desired.
Correction: The proof points to the general phenomenon that prayers are answered, not that every individual petition is granted in the specific form requested. The emphasis is on the observable fact that relief and help occur, especially in times of distress.

Checkpoint

What one tangible step can you take today to purify your heart and reduce attachment to the corporeal?
?
You profess that God hears your prayers, but does your daily life reflect that conviction? When raising your hands in supplication, do you truly see Him as the ultimate goal of all your needs? Or do you sometimes treat prayer as a formality? If 'needs never cease from His presence', how would your relationship with Him change if you really lived in that awareness right now?

The Third Proof: Instinctive Turning to God in Distress
Sanusi presents the third proof: instinctive supplication in distress.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the universal human instinct to supplicate in distress serves as a proof for the existence of God, even among skeptics.

Core Idea
In distress, even skeptics instinctively turn to God, revealing an innate recognition.

This lesson introduces a unique proof from spiritual experience: the spontaneous turning to God during calamities. Even those who deny God find themselves supplicating in genuine distress, showing that sound intellects inherently affirm a higher power. Sanusi supports this with psychological observation and a Quranic verse.

Guided Path
1

The verse is used as evidence for the instinctive response.
4
Consider the conclusion

The conclusion is that all sound intellects affirm the existence of the Wise God.

The third type of proofs of the people of spiritual exercises (ashāb al-riyāḍāt): that they said: Indeed the people of doubts and ambiguities, even if they strive in establishing false fancies and confirming corrupt ambiguities in denying the managing God (al-ilāh al-mudabbir), except that when a painful calamity and a terrifying event befalls them, they find from their clear intellects and hearts: supplication, and displaying humility to the Lord of the world, and asking Him to deliver them from that affliction and bring them out of that trial. Commentary
This passage presents the third type of proof from those who practice spiritual exercises (riyāḍāt). It is a psychological and observational argument. The author states that even people who are deeply entrenched in doubts and false arguments denying the existence of a guiding God (al-ilāh al-mudabbir) undergo a spontaneous change when struck by a painful or terrifying event. At that moment, their clear intellects and hearts naturally turn to supplication, humility, and prayer to the Lord of the world, asking for deliverance. This reaction is so universal that it is known by necessity through induction and reflection. However, once the affliction is removed, they often revert to their doubts and false fancies. The author cites Quran 29:65 to support this, noting that people call upon God sincerely when in danger at sea but fall into shirk when saved. This proves that all sound intellects inherently affirm the existence of the Wise God, even if some later suppress it.

The Empirical Proof from Answered Prayers
Sanusi presents the fourth type: empirical proof from answered prayers and divine assistance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how universal human experience of answered prayers and divine assistance serves as a rational proof for the existence of a responsive God.

Core Idea
Answered prayers are empirical evidence that God exists and hears.

Sanusi introduces a fourth category of proof from the people of spiritual exercises: direct empirical evidence. He argues that everyone, through repeated experience, knows that prayers of the distressed are answered, the grieved are relieved, and the oppressed are helped. This universal observation, he contends, points to a God who hears and responds. In this lesson, we examine this experiential argument and its implications.

1
Read the passage once

The passage uses the phrase 'everyone knows by the validity of experience'.
2
Identify the empirical basis
Look for the specific examples Sanusi gives: answering the distressed, relieving the grieved, helping the oppressed. Notice that these are observable events.
He also mentions a correlation with spiritual purity.
3
Follow the inference to God
After describing the experiences, Sanusi states 'that indicates that this world has a God who hears supplication and answers the call.' See how the empirical observation leads to a theological conclusion.
This is the core of the proof.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider how this proof is different from purely logical or scriptural proofs. It is based on common human experience, making it accessible to everyone.
The commentary notes this is an inductive, not just subjective, proof.
5
Review the key takeaways
Consolidate your understanding by answering the checkpoint question and formulating the key takeaway.
The activity will help you apply the concept to your own experience.

The Argument from Precaution for a Creator
Sanusi presents the argument from precaution by the third group, with a three-step cumulative case.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument from precaution and how it leads to belief in a God who freely acts and issues commands.

Core Idea
Precaution demands belief in a commanding Creator.

This lesson presents an argument based on risk avoidance. Imam al-Sanusi introduces a third group of thinkers who, though not trained in the precise sciences, use their sound intellects to arrive at the existence of a Creator. Their first way is the argument from precaution (ihtiyat), which reasons step by step that affirming a God who commands and forbids is the safest position.

Guided Path
1
Understand the context
Read about the third group: people with complete intellects but no training in precise sciences. They use their natural reason to argue from precaution.
This group is distinct from philosophers and theologians.
2
Grasp the precaution principle
The principle: observing precaution in everything is better than neglecting it. Recognize that this is a practical reasoning tool for situations of uncertainty.
3
Follow the three-step argument
Notice the three nested alternatives: (1) world has a God or not, (2) God is free-acting or not, (3) God commands/forbids or not. For each, affirmation avoids harm if true, and does no harm if false.
The argument builds cumulatively; each step reinforces the previous.
4
See the conclusion
The cumulative reasoning leads to acknowledging a God who is a free-acting agent and who commands and forbids. This is the most precautionary position.
The text ends abruptly, but the conclusion is clear.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الطائفة الثالثة من طوائف أهل العالم : الذين حصلت لهم عقول كاملة وأفهام صحيحة ، إلا أنهم لم يشتغلوا بطلب العلوم الدقيقة . ولقد سمعت من هذا الجنس [من الناس] عبارات صحيحة دالة على إثبات الإله لهذا العالم . فالطريق الأول : قال بعضهم : رعاية الاحتياط في كل شيء ، أولى من إهمال الاحتياط. فنقول : القول بإثبات الإله المختار للمكلف أقرب إلى الاحتياط من القول بنفيه ، فكان الذهاب إلى هذا القول أولى . أما بيان أنه أحوط . فتقريره أن نقول : هذا العالم : إما أن يكون له إله ، وإما أن لا يكون . فإن لم يكن ، كان القول بإثباته مضراً، فثبت أن القول [ بإله العالم (4) أقرب إلى الاحتياط . ثم نقول : إله العالم إما أن يكون فاعلاً مختاراً ، أو لا يكون () ، فإن لم يكن فاعلاً مختاراً كان إثبات الفاعل المختار غير مضر . أما إن كان فاعلاً مختاراً كان نفيه مضراً. فكان إثبات الفاعل المختار أبعد عن الضرر ، وأقرب إلى الاحتياط . ثم نقول : هذا الإله الفاعل المختار . إما أن يقال : إنه كلف العباد ، وأمرهم ببعض الأشياء ، ونهاهم عن بعضها ، وإما أن يقال : إنه ليس كذلك ، فإن لم يكن كذلك لم يلزم من القول بكونه أمرا ناهيا ضرر ، وإن كان كذلك كان إنكار كونه آمرا ناهيا أعظم المضار . فثبت بما ذكرنا : أن الاعتراف بأن لهذا العالم إلها ، وأن ذلك الإله فاعل مختار ، وأنه آمـر نـاهي أبعد عن
The third party of the groups of the people of the world: those who have attained complete intellects and correct understandings, except that they have not occupied themselves with seeking precise sciences. And I have heard from this kind [of people] correct statements indicating the establishment of a God (ilāh) for this world. So the first way: some of them said: Observing precaution (iḥtiyāṭ) in everything is better than neglecting precaution. So we say: The statement affirming a choosing God (al-ilāh al-mukhtār) for the responsible person is closer to precaution than the statement denying Him, so going to this statement is preferable. As for explaining that it is more precautionary, its exposition is that we say: This world: either it has a God, or it does not. If it does not, then affirming Him is harmful (ḍārr), so it is established that the statement [of a God of the world] is closer to precaution. Then we say: The God of the world: either He is a free-acting agent (fāʿil mukhtār), or He is not. If He is not a free-acting agent, then affirming a free-acting agent is not harmful. But if He is a free-acting agent, then denying Him is harmful. So affirming the free-acting agent is further from harm and closer to precaution. Then we say: This God, the free-acting agent: either it is said that He tasked the servants and commanded them with some things and forbade them from some things, or it is said that He is not like that. If He is not like that, then from the statement that He is commanding and forbidding no harm follows; but if He is like that, then denying that He is commanding and forbidding is the greatest of harms. So it is established by what we have mentioned: that the acknowledgment that this world has a God, and that God is a free-acting agent, and that He is commanding and forbidding, is further from
Source pages p. 273

Commentary
This passage introduces a new group: people with complete intellects and correct understandings who have not studied the precise sciences. They produce their own proofs. The first way is the argument from precaution (iḥtiyāṭ). The argument proceeds step by step. First, precaution in general is better than neglecting it. Second, affirming a choosing God for the responsible person is more precautionary than denying Him. To prove this, the author sets up a series of alternatives. First, either the world has a God or it does not. If it does not, affirming Him causes no harm (because there is no God to punish); if it does, denying Him is harmful (because punishment may follow). So affirmation is safer. Second, either this God is a free-acting agent (fāʿil mukhtār) or not. If not, affirming such a God is harmless; if He is, denying Him is harmful. So affirmation is again safer. Third, either this free-acting agent commands and forbids or not. If not, affirming that He does so is harmless; if He does, denying His commands is the greatest harm. Thus, acknowledging a God who is a free-acting agent and who commands and forbids is the most cautious position. The text ends abruptly with 'further from', but the conclusion is clear: the precautionary principle leads to belief in a commanding Creator.

Key Terms
الاحتياط (iḥtiyāṭ)
الإله المختار (al-ilāh al-mukhtār)
الفاعل المختار (al-fāʿil al-mukhtār)
آمر ناه (āmir nāhin)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument from precaution is a cynical or utilitarian calculation, not a sincere epistemic reasoning.
Correction: The argument assumes genuine uncertainty and uses the principle of avoiding potential harm; it is a rational method to reach the safest belief, not a mere calculation of worldly benefit.

Checkpoint
According to the argument from precaution, why is affirming a God who commands and forbids the safest position?
A) Because it is the most popular belief.
B) Because it avoids the great harm of denying a real commanding God while incurring no harm if He does not exist.
C) Because it requires no intellectual effort.
D) Because it is supported by scripture.

Key Takeaway
The argument from precaution shows that affirming a Creator who commands is the rationally safest position because it avoids great potential harm while incurring none if false.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believed that denying the existence of a commanding Creator could lead to the greatest harm, would you not be seeking Him with all your energy? What does your actual pursuit of truth say about your conviction in the precautionary argument?
?
The argument shows that believing in a God who commands is the safest rational position. But are you content with a merely rational safe position, or do you seek to know the reality? If you are only being precautionary, how does that affect your worship?
?
Sanusi presents this argument from people who did not study precise sciences but used their intellect. Have you used your own intellect to examine the question of God's existence, or have you relied on others? What would it mean for you to personally apply the precautionary principle to your life?

The Argument from Caution (Ihtiyat)
Razi presents the argument from caution using three disjunctions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument from caution (ihtiyat) as a pragmatic proof for the existence of God, His free agency, and His commanding and prohibiting.

Core Idea
Caution demands belief in God, free agency, and command.

This proof does not rely on demonstrative reasoning but on practical risk assessment. The idea is that if you are wrong about God, the harm is severe, so it is safer to believe. The argument proceeds through three dichotomies, each step showing that affirming the religious claim is less risky than denying it. Razi attributes this reasoning to people of sound intellect who did not engage in deep theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the logical structure of three successive 'either-or' choices.
The passage has an introduction describing the source of this argument.
2
Understand the principle of caution
The passage begins with the maxim: 'Observing caution in everything is better than neglecting it.' This is the foundation of the argument.
3
Follow the three disjunctions
The argument applies caution to three questions: (1) Does the world have a deity? (2) Is that deity a free agent? (3) Does that deity command and prohibit? In each case, affirming carries no harm if false, but denying carries great harm if true.
Notice the cumulative logic: each step builds on the previous conclusion.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The conclusion is that belief in God, free agency, and divine command is furthest from harm and closest to caution. Because fear of punishment (in the afterlife) is intense, the cautious course is obligatory.
This is not a logical proof but a prudential one.
5
Consider the misunderstanding
The common misunderstanding is that this argument is about worldly benefit. In fact, it is about eschatological harm and fear of punishment.
The text says 'intense fear' implies fear of the afterlife.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فهذه هي الوجوه التي حصلناها من كلمات أصحاب الرياضات. الطائفة الثالثة من طوائف أهل العالم: الذين حصلت لهم عقول كاملة وأفهام صحيحة، إلا أنهم لم يشتغلوا بطلب العلوم الدقيقة. ولقد سمعت من هذا الجنس من الناس عبارات صحيحة دالة على إثبات الإله لهذا العالم.

فالطريق الأول: قال بعضهم: رعاية الاحتياط في كل شيء أولى من إهمال الاحتياط. فنقول: القول بإثبات الإله المختار للمكلف أقرب إلى الاحتياط من القول بنفيه، فكان الذهاب إلى هذا القول أولى. أما بيان أنه أحوط فتقريره أن نقول: هذا العالم إما أن يكون له إله وإما أن لا يكون. فإن لم يكن، كان القول بإثباته غير مضر، وإن كان، كان القول بنفيه مضراً، فثبت أن القول بإله العالم أقرب إلى الاحتياط. ثم نقول: إله العالم إما أن يكون فاعلاً مختاراً أو لا يكون، فإن لم يكن فاعلاً مختاراً كان إثبات الفاعل المختار غير مضر، أما إن كان فاعلاً مختاراً كان نفيه مضراً. فكان إثبات الفاعل المختار أبعد عن الضرر وأقرب إلى الاحتياط. ثم نقول: هذا الإله الفاعل المختار إما أن يقال إنه كلف العباد وأمرهم ببعض الأشياء ونهاهم عن بعضها وإما أن يقال إنه ليس كذلك، فإن لم يكن كذلك لم يلزم من القول بكونه آمراً ناهياً ضرر، وإن كان كذلك كان إنكار كونه آمراً ناهياً أعظم المضار. فثبت بما ذكرنا: أن الاعتراف بأن لهذا العالم إلهاً، وأن ذلك الإله فاعل مختار، وأنه آمر ناهٍ أبعد عن الخوف وأقرب إلى الاحتياط، وإذا كان الأمر كذلك وجب أن يكون المصير إلى هذا المذهب والاعتقاد أحوط. لأن عند استيلاء الخوف الشديد يكون الأخذ بالجانب الأحوط متعيناً. والله ولي التوفيق.
These are the aspects we have obtained from the words of the people of spiritual exercises. The third group of the classes of the people of the world: those who have attained complete intellects and correct understandings, except that they did not occupy themselves with seeking the precise sciences. And I have heard from this kind of people correct expressions indicating the affirmation of the deity for this world.

So the first way: Some of them said: Observing caution in everything is better than neglecting caution. So we say: The statement affirming the chosen deity for the responsible person is closer to caution than the statement denying Him, so adopting this statement is better. As for the explanation that it is more precautionary, its formulation is that we say: This world either has a deity or does not have one. If it does not have one, then the statement affirming Him is not harmful, and if it has one, then the statement denying Him is harmful. So it is established that the statement affirming the deity of the world is closer to caution. Then we say: The deity of the world is either a free agent or not. If He is not a free agent, then affirming the free agent is not harmful. But if He is a free agent, then denying Him is harmful. So affirming the free agent is further from harm and closer to caution. Then we say: This free-agent deity is either said to have obligated the servants, commanded them some things, and prohibited them from some things, or said not to be so. If He is not so, then no harm follows from the statement that He is a commander and prohibitor. But if He is so, then denying that He is a commander and prohibitor is the greatest of harms. So it is established by what we have mentioned: that acknowledging that this world has a deity, that that deity is a free agent, and that He is a commander and prohibitor is further from fear and closer to caution. And if the matter is so, then it is necessary that adopting this doctrine and belief is more precautionary. Because when intense fear prevails, taking the more cautious side is incumbent. And God is the giver of success.
Source pages p. 273

Commentary
This passage presents a proof for the existence of God, His attribute of being a free agent, and His issuing of commands and prohibitions, based on the principle of precaution (ihtiyat). It is taken from the sayings of the third group of people—those with sound intellect but not engaged in deep sciences. The author first states the general maxim: observing caution is better than neglecting it. Then he applies this to three successive disjunctions: (1) The world either has a God or not; if it has a God, disbelief is harmful, while if not, belief is harmless, so belief is more cautious. (2) This God is either a free agent or not; if He is free, denial harms, while if not, affirmation does not harm, so affirming free agency is more cautious. (3) This God either issues commands and prohibitions or not; if He does, denying this harms greatly, while if not, affirming does not harm, so affirming His commanding and prohibiting is more cautious. The conclusion is that the belief that God exists, is a free agent, and commands and prohibits is furthest from harm and closest to caution. Since intense fear (of the afterlife) dominates, the cautious course is incumbent. The proof is not based on logical demonstration but on pragmatic risk assessment, yet it argues that rational precaution alone necessitates belief.

Key Terms
احتياط (ihtiyat - caution)
ضرر (darar - harm)
فاعل مختار (fa'il mukhtar - free agent)
آمر ناه (amir nahi - commander and prohibiter)
مذهب (madhhab - doctrine)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument from caution is about maximizing worldly benefit and pleasure.
Correction: The argument is about avoiding eschatological harm (punishment in the afterlife) due to intense fear, not about worldly gain.

Checkpoint
According to the argument from caution (ihtiyat), what three things must one affirm about God to be most cautious?
A) Existence, omnipotence, and mercy
B) Existence, free agency, and command/prohibition
C) Existence, oneness, and justice
D) Existence, knowledge, and will

Key Takeaway
The argument from caution uses pragmatic risk assessment to justify belief in God, free agency, and divine command as the safest option given the possibility of eternal harm.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly internalized that denying God carries the risk of infinite harm, what would you fear losing in this world that currently holds more sway over your choices than that risk?
?
The argument from caution says that affirming God's commands and prohibitions is safer. But if you examine your daily life, which divine command do you consistently neglect, convinced that the 'safe' path is actually to follow your own desires?
?
Imagine you are standing before God tomorrow and He asks: 'I gave you the argument from caution as a clear sign, yet you still did not act upon it with full commitment.' What would be your honest answer about why you hesitated? What can you do today to make that answer unnecessary?

The Slap Proof for God's Existence
Razi uses the child's response to a slap to prove that an action must have an agent, establishing God's existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a child's instinctive reaction to a slap demonstrates that every event must have an agent, leading to the existence of the Wise Organizer.

Core Idea
A child's question 'Who struck me?' proves that every action needs an agent.

Razi presents a second popular proof using an everyday observation. He argues that a child's immediate 'Who struck me?' reveals a universal truth: pure reason and innate disposition affirm that every event has an originator. This reflection leads to affirming the existence of a wise organizer.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on existence
Read the first two paragraphs of the Arabic passage carefully, focusing on the child's reaction and the logical conclusion drawn by Razi.
Notice how the argument moves from a single slap to the entire cosmos.
2
Identify the child's first question
The child asks 'Who struck me?' This shows that pure reason requires an agent for any action.
3
Understand the universal premise
Razi states that pure reason and fitra testify that every event needs an originator. This is the key logical premise.
This premise is then applied to the whole world.
4
Apply the premise to the cosmos
If a single slap cannot occur without an agent, then the entire universe with all its events certainly cannot exist without a Creator.
This is presented as one of the strongest proofs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الطريق الثاني: كان بعض العقلاء يقول: إن لطمة واحدة تضرب على وجه صبي، تظهر أن لهذا العالم إلهاً، وأن هذا الإله أمر بعض عباده بأشياء ونهاهم عن أشياء، وأن ذلك الإله أعد للمطيعين ثواباً وللمذنبين عقاباً، وأنه بعث إلى الخلق رسلاً مبشرين ومنذرين. وهذه هي الأصول الأربعة التي هي أشرف المطالب وأعز المقاصد.

أما دلالة هذه اللطمة على المطلوب الأول وهو إثبات الإله تعالى فنقول: ذلك الصبي إذا أحس بتلك اللطمة، ففي الحال يصيح ويقول: من الذي ضربني؟ ومن الذي لطم وجهي؟ ولو أن أهل الدنيا يجتمعون عليه ويقولون: إن هذه اللطمة حصلت من غير فاعل، فإنه لا يقبل هذا القول، ولا يؤثر فيه هذا الكلام. وهذا يدل على أن صريح العقل وأول الفطرة شاهدة بأن الفعل لا بد له من فاعل، والحادث لا بد له من محدث. ومتى ظهرت هذه المقدمة فنقول: إذا كان صريح العقل يستبعد حدوث تلك اللطمة من غير فاعل، فحدوث جملة الحوادث في عالم الأفلاك وعالم العناصر، كيف يعقل حدوثها بلا محدث وفاعل؟ فصار هذا الاعتبار من أدل الدلائل على دلالة حدوث هذا العالم على وجود الصانع المدبر.

وأما دلالة هذه اللطمة على المطلوب الثاني وهو كون الإله تعالى موصوفاً بالأمر والنهي والتكليف، فنقول: إن ذلك الصبي إذا عرف أن ذلك الذي لطمه هو فلان، فإنه في الحال يقول: لم ضربتني؟ وبأي سبب آذيتني؟ وهذا يدل على أن صريح عقله حكم بأن الخلق ما تركوا مهملين معطلين، بل التكاليف عليهم لازمة.
The second way: Some rational people used to say: A single slap struck on the face of a child shows that this world has a deity, that this deity commanded some of His servants with things and prohibited them from things, that that deity prepared reward for the obedient and punishment for the sinners, and that He sent to creation messengers as bringers of good news and warners. These are the four principles which are the noblest of goals and dearest of objectives.

As for the indication of this slap to the first objective, which is affirming the exalted deity, we say: That child, when he feels that slap, immediately cries out and says: Who struck me? Who slapped my face? Even if all the people of the world gather upon him and say: This slap occurred without an agent, he does not accept this statement, nor does this speech affect him. And this indicates that pure reason and primary innate disposition testify that an action must have an agent, and an event must have an originator. When this premise is established, we say: If pure reason deems it improbable that that slap occurred without an agent, then the occurrence of the totality of events in the world of celestial spheres and the world of elements—how can their occurrence be conceived without an originator and agent? So this reflection becomes among the strongest proofs for the indication of the occurrence of this world upon the existence of the wise organizer.

As for the indication of this slap to the second objective, which is that the exalted deity is described with command, prohibition, and obligation, we say: That child, when he knows that the one who slapped him is so-and-so, he immediately says: Why did you strike me? For what reason did you harm me? And this indicates that his pure reason judged that creatures are not left neglected and unaccountable, but rather obligations are incumbent upon them.
Source pages p. 274

Commentary
This passage presents a second popular proof for God's existence and moral commands, based on a child's natural reaction to a slap. The author explicitly states that this proof establishes four principles: (1) existence of a deity, (2) that He issues commands and prohibitions, (3) that He rewards and punishes, (4) that He sends messengers. The first part of the argument focuses on proving the first principle: the child's immediate question 'Who struck me?' reveals that pure reason (sarih al-aql) and primary innate disposition (awwal al-fitrah) testify that every event must have an agent. The author then applies this universal principle: if a single slap cannot be without an agent, then the entire cosmos, with its celestial and elemental events, certainly cannot exist without an originator and agent. This is presented as one of the strongest proofs for the existence of a wise organizer. The second part proves that God is characterized by command, prohibition, and obligation. When the child knows who slapped him, he immediately asks 'Why did you strike me?', showing that human reason inherently recognizes that actions have reasons and that humans are not left neglected; rather, obligations (takalif) are incumbent upon them. This implies that the deity must have issued commands and prohibitions.

Key Terms
لطمة (latma - slap)
صريح العقل (sarih al-aql - pure reason)
فطرة (fitra - innate disposition)
فاعل (fa'il - agent)
محدث (muhdith - originator)
الصانع المدبر (al-sani' al-mudabbir - the wise organizer)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument is merely anecdotal or emotional and lacks logical force.
Correction: The argument is grounded in a universal principle of reason (every event needs a cause), which is then applied from a small case to the entire cosmos, making it a logical deduction.

Checkpoint
What does the child's first question ('Who struck me?') prove according to the passage?
A) That the child is scared
B) That an action must have an agent
C) That the slapper is unjust
D) That the slap was painful

Key Takeaway
A child's instinctive demand for an agent proves that every event requires a cause, and by extension, the universe requires a Creator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When pain or difficulty strikes you, do you instinctively cry out to the One who allowed it, or do you merely seek a physical explanation? How does your immediate reaction reflect your belief in a personal Creator?
?
The slap proof shows that even a child knows every event needs an Agent. Do you live your daily life as if your actions truly matter to that Agent, or do you sometimes act as if no one is watching? What would change if you took this truth seriously right now?
?
If you were to stand before the Wise Organizer tomorrow, could you honestly say you have acknowledged Him as the Agent of every detail in your life? What one step can you take today to live more consciously in His presence?

The Slap Proof for Divine Commands and Obligations
Razi uses the child's second question to prove that God is characterized by command, prohibition, and obligation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the child's second question ('Why did you strike me?') demonstrates that humans are not left without obligations and that God must have issued commands and prohibitions.

Core Idea
The child's 'Why?' reveals that humans are not abandoned; obligations are inherent.

After establishing God's existence, Razi extends the argument to show that the same reason that demands an agent also demands moral accountability. The child's immediate 'Why did you do that?' proves that pure reason judges that actions have reasons and that creatures are not neglected. This establishes that God is a commander and obligator.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on obligation
Read the third paragraph of the Arabic passage, focusing on the child's second question and Razi's conclusion.
This part builds on the first argument but adds a new dimension.
2
Identify the child's second question
The child asks 'Why did you strike me?' (لم ضربتني) – this shows that reason asks for a purpose or justification.
3
Understand the implication
Razi says this proves that humans are not left neglected (مهملين معطلين) but have obligations (تكاليف) imposed on them.
The child's demand for a reason implies that actions must have a purpose, and thus God must have a purpose in creating and commanding.
4
Connect to divine attributes
This establishes that God is described with command, prohibition, and obligation (الأمر والنهي والتكليف).
This is the second of the four principles mentioned at the start.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The child's question 'Why?' reveals that you know deep down that harm must have a reason. Now look at your own life: when you sin, do you honestly feel accountable to God, or do you brush it off as 'no big deal'? What would it mean to take that accountability seriously starting today?
?
If your pure reason judges that you are not abandoned to do as you please, then why do you sometimes act as if God's commands don't apply to you? Name one area where you have been living as if you have no obligations—and commit right now to change it.
?
You demand reasons from others when they hurt you, but do you ever question yourself about why you disobey God? Write down one command of God you have been ignoring, and then ask yourself: what is the real reason you have not obeyed? Now decide to obey it today.

The Slap Proof: Innate Reason Recognizes a Creator
Sanusi presents a thought experiment showing that reason demands a cause for every event, leading to proof of the Creator.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the child's reaction to a slap demonstrates that every event requires an agent, and how this principle extends to prove the existence of the Creator of the universe.

Core Idea
The instinctive demand for 'who did this?' reveals that reason requires a cause for every event.

In this passage, Sanusi presents a powerful thought experiment. A child is slapped, and his immediate reaction—asking 'who struck me?'—reveals an innate rational principle: every event has an agent. By extending this principle, Sanusi builds a compelling argument for the existence of God as the Creator of all contingent beings.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation to grasp the argument's flow.
2
Analyze the child's reaction
Notice that the child's question shows an instinctive attribution of the slap to an agent, rejecting the idea that it happened by itself.
This demonstrates fitrah (primal nature).
3
Understand the extension to the universe
Sanusi argues that if a single slap cannot be uncaused, how much less can the entire universe be without a Creator?
This is an argument from analogy and gradation.
4
Connect to the overall proof
Reflect on how this sub-proof fits into the larger argument for God's existence based on the world's temporal origination.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما دلالة هذه اللطمة على المطلوب الأول وهو إثبات الإله تعالى فنقول : ذلك الصبي إذا أحس بتلك اللطمة ، ففي الحال يصيح ويقول : من الذي ضربني ؟ ومن الذي لطم وجهي ؟ ولو أن أهل الدنيا يجتمعون عليه ويقولون : إن هذه اللطمة حصلت [بنفسها] من غير فاعل ، فإنه لا يقبل هذا القول ، ولا يؤثر فيه هذا الكلام، وهذا يدل على أن صريح العقل [وأول الفطرة ، شاهدة بأن الفعل] لا بد له من فاعل والحادث لا بد له من محدث ، ومتى ظهرت هذه المقدمة فنقول : إذا كان صريح العقل يستبعد حدوث تلك اللطمة من غير فاعل ، فحدوث جملة الحوادث في عالم الأفلاك ، وعالم العناصر ، كيف يعقل حدوثها بلا محدث وفاعل ؟ فصار هذا الاعتبار من أدل الدلائل على دلالة حدوث هذا العالم ، على وجود الصانع المدبر .
As for the indication of this slap to the first sought object, which is affirming the existence of God the Exalted, we say: That child, when he feels that slap, immediately screams and says: Who struck me? And who slapped my face? And if all the people of the world gathered upon him and said: This slap occurred by itself without a doer, he would not accept this statement, nor would this speech affect him. This indicates that pure reason and primal innate disposition bear witness that an action must have an agent and an event must have an originator. Once this premise is established, we say: If pure reason deems it improbable for that single slap to occur without an agent, then how can the occurrence of all events in the celestial sphere and the elemental world be intelligible without an originator and agent? Thus this consideration becomes among the strongest proofs for the indication of the temporal origination of this world upon the existence of the Wise Creator.
Source pages p. 274

Commentary
This passage presents the first sub-proof of the second path, demonstrating how the slap proves the existence of God. The author uses a thought experiment: when a child is slapped, his immediate reaction is to demand 'who struck me?' Even if everyone around him insists the slap happened by itself, the child's innate reason rejects this. This shows that pure reason (صريح العقل) and primal nature (أول الفطرة) intuitively know that every event requires a cause. The author then extends this principle: if a single slap cannot happen without an agent, then how can the entire universe with all its events arise without a Creator? Thus, the child's instinctive reasoning becomes a powerful analogy for the cosmological argument from contingency and temporal origination (حدوث). The conclusion is that this consideration is among the strongest proofs for the existence of the Wise Organizer.

Key Terms
صريح العقل
أول الفطرة
فاعل
محدث
حدوث
صانع مدبر
